href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">cleanup</a></b> to meet Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style">quality standards</a>. The specific problem is: <b>Needs better presentation and content summarization.</b><span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Liberalism&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> if you can.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2017</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1138" data-file-width="984" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_Locke.jpg/170px-John_Locke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_Locke.jpg/255px-John_Locke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_Locke.jpg/340px-John_Locke.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, who was the first to develop a liberal philosophy, including the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">right to private property</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a></div></div></div>
<p>Isolated strands of liberal thought had existed in <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a> since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" title="Ancient Greeks">Ancient Greeks</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_philosophy" title="Eastern philosophy">Eastern philosophy</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> period. These ideas were first drawn together and systematized as a distinct ideology, by the English philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, generally regarded as the father of modern liberalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delaney,_p._18_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney,_p._18-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Godwin_et_al.,_p._12_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Godwin_et_al.,_p._12-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FungCambridge_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FungCambridge-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BevirSAGE_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-BevirSAGE-38">[38]</a></sup> The first major signs of liberal politics emerged in modern times. These ideas began to coalesce at the time of the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil Wars</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Levellers" title="Levellers">Levellers</a>, a radical political movement, during the war called for <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Freedom of religion in the United Kingdom">freedom of religion</a>, frequent convening of parliament and equality under the law. The impact of these ideas steadily increased during the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Britain" title="Early modern Britain">17th century in England</a>, culminating in the Glorious Revolution of 1688, which enshrined <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty" title="Parliamentary sovereignty">parliamentary sovereignty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Right_of_revolution" title="Right of revolution">right of revolution</a> and led to the establishment of what many consider the first modern, liberal state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The development of liberalism continued throughout the 18th century with the burgeoning Enlightenment ideals of the era. This was a period of profound intellectual vitality that questioned old traditions and influenced several <a href="/wiki/Monarchies_in_Europe" title="Monarchies in Europe">European monarchies</a> throughout the 18th century. Political tension between England and its <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">American colonies</a> grew after 1765 and the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a> over the issue of <a href="/wiki/No_taxation_without_representation" title="No taxation without representation">taxation without representation</a>, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> of a new republic, and the resulting <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> to defend it. After the war, the leaders debated about how to move forward. The <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a>, written in 1776, now appeared inadequate to provide security, or even a functional government. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_in_Congress_Assembled" title="United States in Congress Assembled">Confederation Congress</a> called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention" title="Philadelphia Convention">Constitutional Convention</a> in 1787, which resulted in the writing of a new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution of the United States</a> establishing a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federal</a> government. In the context of the times, the Constitution was a republican and liberal document.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> It remains the oldest liberal governing document in effect worldwide.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Montesquieu_1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1447" data-file-width="1177" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Montesquieu_1.png/170px-Montesquieu_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Montesquieu_1.png/255px-Montesquieu_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Montesquieu_1.png/340px-Montesquieu_1.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Montesquieu_1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, who argued for the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of the powers of government</a></div></div></div>
<p>In Europe, liberalism has a long tradition dating back to the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-160">[160]</a></sup> The French Revolution began in 1789. The two key events that marked the triumph of liberalism were the <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_feudalism_in_France" title="Abolition of feudalism in France">abolition of feudalism in France</a> on the night of 4 August 1789, which marked the collapse of feudal and old traditional rights and privileges and restrictions as well as the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a> in August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, the French brought to Western Europe the liquidation of the <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal system</a>, the liberalization of <a href="/wiki/Property_law" title="Property law">property laws</a>, the end of <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">seigneurial dues</a>, the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a>, the legalization of <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a>, the disintegration of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_ghettos_in_Europe" title="Jewish ghettos in Europe">Jewish ghettos</a>, the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition" title="Spanish Inquisition">Inquisition</a>, the final end of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, the elimination of church courts and religious authority, the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric system</a> and equality under the law for all men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> His most lasting achievement, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_code" title="Napoleonic code">Civil Code</a>, served as "an object of emulation all over the globe",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> but it also perpetuated further discrimination against women under the banner of the "natural order".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>The development into maturity of classical liberalism took place before and after the French Revolution in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vincent,_pp._29–30_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent,_pp._29–30-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wealth_of_Nations" title="The Wealth of Nations">The Wealth of Nations</a></i>, published in 1776, was to provide most of the ideas of economics at least until the publication of <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>'s <i>Principles</i> in 1848.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_pp._63,_68_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_pp._63,_68-94">[94]</a></sup> Smith addressed the motivation for economic activity, the causes of prices and the distribution of wealth and the policies the state should follow in order to maximise wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_p._64_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_p._64-95">[95]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radicalism_(historical)" title="Radicalism (historical)">radical liberal movement</a> began in the 1790s in England and concentrated on parliamentary and electoral reform, emphasizing natural rights and <a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty" title="Popular sovereignty">popular sovereignty</a>. Radicals like <a href="/wiki/Richard_Price" title="Richard Price">Richard Price</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> saw parliamentary reform as a first step toward dealing with their many grievances, including the treatment of <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">Protestant Dissenters</a>, the slave trade, high prices and high taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_and_conservatism_in_Latin_America" title="Liberalism and conservatism in Latin America">Latin America</a>, liberal unrest dates back to the 18th century, when liberal agitation in Latin America led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_American_wars_of_independence" title="Latin American wars of independence">independence</a> from the imperial power of Spain and Portugal. The new regimes were generally liberal in their political outlook and employed the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Positivism" title="Positivism">positivism</a>, which emphasized the truth of modern science, to buttress their positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> In the United States, a <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">vicious war</a> ensured the integrity of the nation and the abolition of slavery in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a>. Historian Don Doyle has argued that the Union victory in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861–1865) gave a major boost to the course of liberalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>During 19th and early 20th century in the Ottoman Empire and Middle East, liberalism influenced periods of reform such as the <a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Nahda" title="Al-Nahda">Al-Nahda</a>; the rise of secularism, constitutionalism and nationalism; and different intellectuals and religious group and movements, like the <a href="/wiki/Young_Ottomans" title="Young Ottomans">Young Ottomans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Modernism" title="Islamic Modernism">Islamic Modernism</a>. Prominent of the era were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rifa%27a_al-Tahtawi" title="Rifa'a al-Tahtawi">Rifa'a al-Tahtawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nam%C4%B1k_Kemal" title="Namık Kemal">Namık Kemal</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0brahim_%C5%9Einasi" title="İbrahim Şinasi">İbrahim Şinasi</a>. However, the reformist ideas and trends did not reach the common population successfully as the books, periodicals and newspapers were accessible primarily to intellectuals and segments of an emerging middle class while many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> saw them as foreign influences on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberalism_and_progressivism_in_the_Muslim_world" title="Liberalism and progressivism in the Muslim world">world of Islam</a>. That perception complicated reformist efforts made by Middle Eastern states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abdelmoula_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abdelmoula-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> These changes, along with other factors, helped to create a sense of crisis within Islam, which continues to this day. This led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_revivalism" title="Islamic revivalism">Islamic revivalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LindgrenRoss_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-LindgrenRoss-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2480" data-file-width="3133" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg/220px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg/330px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg/440px-Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_La_libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The iconic painting <i><a href="/wiki/Liberty_Leading_the_People" title="Liberty Leading the People">Liberty Leading the People</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a>, a tableau of the <a href="/wiki/July_Revolution" title="July Revolution">July Revolution</a> in 1830</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">Abolitionist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrage</a> movements spread, along with representative and democratic ideals. France established an <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">enduring republic</a> in the 1870s. However, nationalism also spread rapidly after 1815. A mixture of liberal and nationalist sentiment in <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_and_radicalism_in_Italy" title="Liberalism and radicalism in Italy">Italy</a> and Germany brought about the unification of the two countries in the late 19th century. A liberal regime came to power in Italy and ended the secular power of the Popes. However, the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Vatican</a> launched a counter crusade against liberalism. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_IX" title="Pope Pius IX">Pope Pius IX</a> issued the <i><a href="/wiki/Syllabus_of_Errors" title="Syllabus of Errors">Syllabus of Errors</a></i> in 1864, condemning liberalism in all its forms. In many countries, liberal forces responded by <a href="/wiki/Suppression_of_the_Society_of_Jesus" title="Suppression of the Society of Jesus">expelling the Jesuit order</a>. By the end of the nineteenth century, the principles of classical liberalism were being increasingly challenged and the ideal of the self-made individual seemed increasingly implausible. Victorian writers like <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a> were early influential critics of social injustice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richardson,_pp._36–37_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson,_pp._36–37-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1909" data-file-width="1400" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg.jpg/170px-Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg.jpg/255px-Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg.jpg/340px-Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaarlo_Juho_St%C3%A5hlberg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_nationalist" title="Liberal nationalist">liberal nationalist</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K._J._St%C3%A5hlberg" title="K. J. Ståhlberg">K. J. Ståhlberg</a> (1865–1952), the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Finland" title="President of Finland">President of Finland</a>, anchored the state in <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>, guarded the fragile germ of the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>, and embarked on internal reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Liberalism gained momentum in the beginning of the 20th century. The bastion of <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocracy</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholas_II" title="Nicholas II">Russian Tsar</a>, was overthrown in the <a href="/wiki/February_Revolution" title="February Revolution">first phase</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>. The Allied victory in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a> and the collapse of four empires seemed to mark the triumph of liberalism across the European continent, not just among the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">victorious allies</a>, but also in Germany and the newly created states of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>. Militarism, as typified by Germany, was defeated and discredited. As Blinkhorn argues, the liberal themes were ascendant in terms of "cultural pluralism, religious and ethnic toleration, national self-determination, free market economics, representative and responsible government, free trade, unionism, and the peaceful settlement of international disputes through a new body, the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>".
</p><p>In the Middle East, liberalism led to constitutional periods, like the Ottoman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Constitutional_Era_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="First Constitutional Era (Ottoman Empire)">First</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Constitutional_Era_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Second Constitutional Era (Ottoman Empire)">Second Constitutional Era</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Constitutional_Revolution" title="Persian Constitutional Revolution">Persian constitutional period</a>, but it declined in the late 1930s due to the growth and opposition of <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pan-Arabism" title="Pan-Arabism">pan-Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">nationalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> However, there were various examples of intellectuals who advocated liberal values and ideas. Prominent liberals during the period were <a href="/wiki/Taha_Hussein" title="Taha Hussein">Taha Hussein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Lutfi_el-Sayed" title="Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed">Ahmed Lutfi el-Sayed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tawfiq_al-Hakim" title="Tawfiq al-Hakim">Tawfiq al-Hakim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abd_El-Razzak_El-Sanhuri" title="Abd El-Razzak El-Sanhuri">Abd El-Razzak El-Sanhuri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Mandur" title="Muhammad Mandur">Muhammad Mandur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FDR_1944_Color_Portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3969" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/FDR_1944_Color_Portrait.jpg/170px-FDR_1944_Color_Portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/FDR_1944_Color_Portrait.jpg/255px-FDR_1944_Color_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/FDR_1944_Color_Portrait.jpg/340px-FDR_1944_Color_Portrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FDR_1944_Color_Portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the United States, <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">modern liberalism</a> traces its history to the popular presidency of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, who initiated the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> in response to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression</a> and won an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">unprecedented four elections</a>. The <a href="/wiki/New_Deal_coalition" title="New Deal coalition">New Deal coalition</a> established by Roosevelt left a decisive legacy and influenced many future American presidents, including <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the definitive liberal response to the Great Depression was given by the British economist <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a>, who had begun a theoretical work examining the relationship between unemployment, money and prices back in the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> The worldwide Great Depression, starting in 1929, hastened the discrediting of liberal economics and strengthened calls for state control over economic affairs. Economic woes prompted widespread unrest in the European political world, leading to the rise of <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a> as an ideology and a movement arrayed against both liberalism and <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> The rise of fascism in the 1930s eventually culminated in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the deadliest conflict in human history. The <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> prevailed in the war by 1945 and their victory set the stage for the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">Communist</a> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> and the liberal <a href="/wiki/Western_Bloc" title="Western Bloc">Western Bloc</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_Iran" title="Liberalism in Iran">In Iran</a>, liberalism enjoyed wide popularity. In April 1951, the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(Iran)" title="National Front (Iran)">National Front</a> became the governing coalition when democratically elected <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Mosaddegh" title="Mohammad Mosaddegh">Mohammad Mosaddegh</a>, a liberal nationalist, took office as the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Iran" title="Prime Minister of Iran">Prime Minister</a>. However, his way of governing entered in conflict with Western interest and he was removed from power in a <a href="/wiki/1953_Iranian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Iranian coup d'état">coup on 19 August 1953</a>. The coup ended the dominance of liberalism in the country's politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the various regional and national movements, the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> in the United States during the 1960s strongly highlighted the liberal efforts for <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">equal rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_Society" title="Great Society">Great Society</a> project launched by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> oversaw the creation of <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(United_States)" title="Medicare (United States)">Medicare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medicaid" title="Medicaid">Medicaid</a>, the establishment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_Start_Program" title="Head Start Program">Head Start</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Job_Corps" title="Job Corps">Job Corps</a> as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_on_Poverty" title="War on Poverty">War on Poverty</a> and the passage of the landmark <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a>, an altogether rapid series of events that some historians have dubbed the "Liberal Hour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nizhny_Novgorod._Anti-Corruption_Rally_(26_March_2017).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2802" data-file-width="4204" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Nizhny_Novgorod._Anti-Corruption_Rally_%2826_March_2017%29.jpg/220px-Nizhny_Novgorod._Anti-Corruption_Rally_%2826_March_2017%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Nizhny_Novgorod._Anti-Corruption_Rally_%2826_March_2017%29.jpg/330px-Nizhny_Novgorod._Anti-Corruption_Rally_%2826_March_2017%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Nizhny_Novgorod._Anti-Corruption_Rally_%2826_March_2017%29.jpg/440px-Nizhny_Novgorod._Anti-Corruption_Rally_%2826_March_2017%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nizhny_Novgorod._Anti-Corruption_Rally_(26_March_2017).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/2017%E2%80%932018_Russian_protests" title="2017–2018 Russian protests">2017–2018 Russian protests</a> were organized by Russia's liberal opposition.</div></div></div>
<p>The Cold War featured extensive ideological competition and several <a href="/wiki/Proxy_war" title="Proxy war">proxy wars</a>, but the widely feared <a href="/wiki/World_War_III" title="World War III">World War III</a> between the Soviet Union and the United States never occurred. While communist states and liberal democracies competed against one another, an <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">economic crisis</a> in the 1970s inspired a move away from <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian economics</a>, especially under <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> in the United Kingdom and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> in the United States. This trend, known as <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a>, constituted a <a href="/wiki/Paradigm_shift" title="Paradigm shift">paradigm shift</a> away from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-war_Keynesian_consensus" title="Post-war Keynesian consensus">post-war Keynesian consensus</a> which had lasted from 1945 to 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Meanwhile, nearing the end of the 20th century, communist states in Eastern Europe <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">collapsed precipitously</a>, leaving liberal democracies as the only major forms of government in the West.
</p><p>At the beginning of World War II, the number of democracies around the world was about the same as it had been forty years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> After 1945, liberal democracies spread very quickly, but then retreated. In <i>The Spirit of Democracy</i>, Larry Diamond argues that by 1974 "dictatorship, not democracy, was the way of the world" and that "barely a quarter of independent states chose their governments through competitive, free, and fair elections". Diamond goes on to say that democracy bounced back and by 1995 the world was "predominantly democratic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_and_support">Criticism and support</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fusilamiento_de_Torrijos_(Gisbert).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1988" data-file-width="3051" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Fusilamiento_de_Torrijos_%28Gisbert%29.jpg/220px-Fusilamiento_de_Torrijos_%28Gisbert%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Fusilamiento_de_Torrijos_%28Gisbert%29.jpg/330px-Fusilamiento_de_Torrijos_%28Gisbert%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Fusilamiento_de_Torrijos_%28Gisbert%29.jpg/440px-Fusilamiento_de_Torrijos_%28Gisbert%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fusilamiento_de_Torrijos_(Gisbert).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Torrijos_and_his_Companions_on_the_Beach_at_M%C3%A1laga" title="Execution of Torrijos and his Companions on the Beach at Málaga">Execution of José María de Torrijos y Uriarte and his men</a> in 1831 as Spanish King <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_VII_of_Spain" title="Ferdinand VII of Spain">Ferdinand VII</a> took <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Spain_(1810%E2%80%9373)#The_Ominous_Decade_(1823–1833)" title="History of Spain (1810–73)">repressive measures</a> against the liberal forces in his country</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raif_Badawi_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1614" data-file-width="1506" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Raif_Badawi_cropped.jpg/170px-Raif_Badawi_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Raif_Badawi_cropped.jpg/255px-Raif_Badawi_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Raif_Badawi_cropped.jpg/340px-Raif_Badawi_cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raif_Badawi_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Raif_Badawi" title="Raif Badawi">Raif Badawi</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabian</a> writer and the creator of the website Free Saudi Liberals, who was sentenced to ten years in prison and 1,000 lashes for "insulting Islam" in 2014</div></div></div>
<p>Liberalism has drawn both criticism and support in its history from various ideological groups. Less friendly to the goals of liberalism has been <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a>, considered by some to be the first major proponent of modern conservative thought, offered a blistering critique of the French Revolution by assailing the liberal pretensions to the power of rationality and to the natural equality of all humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby,_p._108_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grigsby,_p._108-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some confusion remains about the relationship between social liberalism and <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>, despite the fact that many variants of socialism distinguish themselves markedly from liberalism by <a href="/wiki/Anti-capitalism" title="Anti-capitalism">opposing capitalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a>. Socialism formed as a group of related yet divergent ideologies in the 19th century such as <a href="/wiki/Christian_socialism" title="Christian socialism">Christian socialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a> (with the writings of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Social_anarchism" title="Social anarchism">social anarchism</a> (with the writings of <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a>), the latter two influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a>. These ideologies—as with liberalism and conservatism—fractured into several major and minor movements in the following decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Marx rejected the foundational aspects of liberal theory, hoping to destroy both the state and the liberal distinction between society and the individual while fusing the two into a collective whole designed to overthrow the developing capitalist order of the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Today, socialist parties and ideas remain a political force with varying degrees of power and influence on all continents leading national governments in many countries.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> stated that—in contrast with <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>—liberal science defends <a href="/wiki/Wage_slavery" title="Wage slavery">wage slavery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> However, some proponents of liberalism like <a href="/wiki/George_Henry_Evans" title="George Henry Evans">George Henry Evans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Gesell" title="Silvio Gesell">Silvio Gesell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a> were critics of wage slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> One of the most outspoken critics of liberalism was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> which resulted in lengthy power struggles between national governments and the Church. In the same vein, conservatives have also attacked what they perceive to be the reckless liberal pursuit of progress and material gains, arguing that such preoccupations undermine traditional social values rooted in community and continuity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> However, a few variations of conservatism, like <a href="/wiki/Liberal_conservatism" title="Liberal conservatism">liberal conservatism</a>, expound some of the same ideas and principles championed by classical liberalism, including "small government and thriving capitalism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grigsby,_p._108_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grigsby,_p._108-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">Social democracy</a>, an ideology advocating progressive modification of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>, emerged in the 20th century and was influenced by socialism. Broadly defined as a project that aims to correct through government reformism what it regards as the intrinsic defects of capitalism by reducing inequalities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> social democracy was also not against the state. Several commentators have noted strong similarities between social liberalism and social democracy, with one political scientist even calling American liberalism "bootleg social democracy" due to the absence of a significant social democratic tradition in the United States that liberals have tried to rectify.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> Another movement associated with modern democracy, <a href="/wiki/Christian_democracy" title="Christian democracy">Christian democracy</a>, hopes to spread <a href="/wiki/Catholic_social_teaching" title="Catholic social teaching">Catholic social ideas</a> and has gained a large following in some European nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> The early roots of Christian democracy developed as a reaction against the <a href="/wiki/Industrialisation" title="Industrialisation">industrialisation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urbanisation" title="Urbanisation">urbanisation</a> associated with <i>laissez-faire</i> liberalism in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> Despite these complex relationships, some scholars have argued that liberalism actually "rejects ideological thinking" altogether, largely because such thinking could lead to unrealistic expectations for human society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fascism_and_ideology#Liberalism" title="Fascism and ideology">Fascists accuse liberalism</a> of materialism and a lack of spiritual values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-massaschussetts1_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-massaschussetts1-209">[209]</a></sup> In particular, fascism opposes liberalism for its <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revolution1994_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-revolution1994-210">[210]</a></sup> Fascists believe that the liberal emphasis on individual freedom produces national divisiveness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-massaschussetts1_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-massaschussetts1-209">[209]</a></sup> but many fascists agree with liberals in their support of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_property_rights" title="Private property rights">private property rights</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-revolution1994_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-revolution1994-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">Leftists</a> accuse the economic doctrines of liberalism, such as economic individual freedom, of giving rise to what they view as a system of exploitation that goes against democratic principles of liberalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-211">[211]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">Right-wingers</a> accuse the social doctrines of liberalism, such as secularism and individual rights, of breaking down communities and dissolving the social fabric that they believe a country needs to prosper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars have praised the influence of liberal internationalism, claiming that the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globalisation" title="Globalisation">globalisation</a> "constitutes a triumph of the liberal vision that first appeared in the eighteenth century" while also writing that liberalism is "the only comprehensive and hopeful vision of world affairs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Russian President <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>, as reported in the <i><a href="/wiki/Financial_Times" title="Financial Times">Financial Times</a></i>, "liberalism has become obsolete".  He claims that the vast majority of people in the world oppose multiculturalism, immigration, and rights for people who are LGBT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> criticized that liberalization would destroy political stability of the People's Republic of China and Chinese Communist Party, making it difficult for development to take place, and is inherently capitalistic. He termed it as <a href="/wiki/Bourgeois_liberalization" title="Bourgeois liberalization">bourgeois liberalization</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"With a nod to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Trivers" title="Robert Trivers">Robert Trivers</a>' definition of altruistic behaviour" (<a href="#CITEREFTrivers1971">Trivers 1971</a>, p. 35), <a href="/wiki/Satoshi_Kanazawa" title="Satoshi Kanazawa">Satoshi Kanazawa</a> defines liberalism (as opposed to conservatism) as "the genuine concern for the welfare of genetically unrelated others and the willingness to contribute larger proportions of private resources for the welfare of such others" (<a href="#CITEREFKanazawa2010">Kanazawa 2010</a>, p. 38).</span>

<li id="cite_note-John_Joseph_Lalor-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-John_Joseph_Lalor_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFLalor1883"><a href="/wiki/John_Joseph_Lalor" title="John Joseph Lalor">Lalor, John Joseph</a> (1883). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/cyclopaediapoli00lalogoog" rel="nofollow"><i>Cyclopædia of Political Science, Political Economy, and of the Political History of the United States</i></a>. Nabu Press. p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/cyclopaediapoli00lalogoog/page/n758" rel="nofollow">760</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">31 December</span> 2007</span>. <q>Democracy attaches itself to a form of government: liberalism, to liberty and guarantees of liberty. The two may agree; they are not contradictory, but they are neither identical, nor necessarily connected. In the moral order, liberalism is the liberty to think, recognised and practiced. This is primordial liberalism, as the liberty to think is itself the first and noblest of liberties. Man would not be free in any degree or in any sphere of action, if he were not a thinking being endowed with consciousness. The freedom of worship, the freedom of education, and the freedom of the press are derived the most directly from the freedom to think.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Cyclop%C3%A6dia+of+Political+Science%2C+Political+Economy%2C+and+of+the+Political+History+of+the+United+States&amp;rft.pages=760&amp;rft.pub=Nabu+Press&amp;rft.date=1883&amp;rft.aulast=Lalor&amp;rft.aufirst=John+Joseph&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fcyclopaediapoli00lalogoog&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALiberalism"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFConway2014">Conway, Martin (2014). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ECIfAwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA184" rel="nofollow">"The Limits of an Anti-liberal Europe"</a>.  In Gosewinkel, Dieter (ed.). <i>Anti-liberal Europe: A Neglected Story of Europeanization</i>. Berghahn Books. p. 184. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-78238-426-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-78238-426-7"><bdi>978-1-78238-426-7</bdi></a>. <q>Liberalism, liberal values and liberal institutions formed an integral part of that process of European consolidation. Fifteen years after the end of the Second World War, the liberal and democratic identity of Western Europe had been reinforced on almost all sides by the definition of the West as a place of freedom. Set against the oppression in the Communist East, by the slow development of a greater understanding of the moral horror of Nazism, and by the engagement of intellectuals and others with the new states (and social and political systems) emerging in the non-European world to the South</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=The+Limits+of+an+Anti-liberal+Europe&amp;rft.btitle=Anti-liberal+Europe%3A+A+Neglected+Story+of+Europeanization&amp;rft.pages=184&amp;rft.pub=Berghahn+Books&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-78238-426-7&amp;rft.aulast=Conway&amp;rft.aufirst=Martin&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DECIfAwAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA184&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALiberalism"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-214">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20220227203520/http://www.qstheory.cn/books/2019-07/31/c_1119485398_58.htm" rel="nofollow">"《邓小平文选第三卷》《在党的十二届六中全会上的讲话》"</a> (in Chinese). Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.qstheory.cn/books/2019-07/31/c_1119485398_58.htm" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 27 February 2022<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 February</span> 2022</span>. <q>大家可以回想一下，粉碎“四人帮”以后，全国人大在一九八○年通过一个议案，取消宪法中的关于“大鸣、大放、大辩论、大字报”这一条。为什么做这件事？因为有一股自由化思潮。搞自由化，就会破坏我们安定团结的政治局面。没有一个安定团结的政治局面，就不可能搞建设。<br/>自由化本身就是资产阶级的，没有什么无产阶级的、社会主义的自由化，自由化本身就是对我们现行政策、现行制度的对抗，或者叫反对，或者叫修改。实际情况是，搞自由化就是要把我们引导到资本主义道路上去，所以我们用反对资产阶级自由化这个提法。管什么这里用过、那里用过，无关重要，现实政治要求我们在决议中写这个。我主张用。</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=%E3%80%8A%E9%82%93%E5%B0%8F%E5%B9%B3%E6%96%87%E9%80%89%E7%AC%AC%E4%B8%89%E5%8D%B7%E3%80%8B%E3%80%8A%E5%9C%A8%E5%85%9A%E7%9A%84%E5%8D%81%E4%BA%8C%E5%B1%8A%E5%85%AD%E4%B8%AD%E5%85%A8%E4%BC%9A%E4%B8%8A%E7%9A%84%E8%AE%B2%E8%AF%9D%E3%80%8B&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.qstheory.cn%2Fbooks%2F2019-07%2F31%2Fc_1119485398_58.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALiberalism"></span></span>
</li>
